SILSOE v. MAULDEN.—The return match was played in Wrest Park, Silsoe, on Thursday afternoon, Silsoe again proving victorious by nearly 100 runs on the first innings. For the winners, J. Gray made 30, M. Squire 23, H. Squire 19, and A. Bottoms 16 not out. Maulden were dismissed for 19, Gudgin taking 6 wickets for 6 runs, and Porter 3 for 10 runs. In their second innings, Maulden compiled 37 for 8 wickets.
HOUGHTON C.C.: MARRIED v. SINGLE.—Played at Houghton on Monday. Score:—Single, 39; Married, 100 for seven wickets.
MESSRS FORDER'S EMPLOYEES.—A match between the employees of Sewell Lime Works and Blows Down Lime Works was played at Houghton Green on Saturday, and resulted in a win for the Sewell men by one run, the scores being 61 and 60 respectively.
WATERLOW'S v ST. MATTHEW'S (LUTON).—Played at Luton on Saturday. Score:—St. Matthew's, 61; Waterlow's, 114.
DUNSTABLE 2ND XI. v. MARKYATE STREET.—Played at Cheverells on Saturday. Rain considerably interfered with the play, and when Dunstable had made 70 for the loss of two wickets, the villagers declared they had had enough of it, and stumps were drawn.

HOUGHTON O.C. MARRIED v. SINGLE.—Played at Houghton on Monday and ended in favour of the married team. Score:—Single: H Tompson b Buckingham, 13; T Evans run out, 0; J Robinson b Buckingham, 0; A Cook b Buckingham, 0; W Carter not out, 8; W Evans b Buckingham, 0; W Musket run out, 0; H Ward b Holt, 0; F Groom run out, 3; H Blaney b Buckingham, 3; W Richardson b Holt, 1; Extras, 11; total, 39.—Married: G Oogswell b Robinson, 5; A Calcott b Tompson, 0; F Fensome c Carter b Tompson, 19; W Buckingham not out, 43; J Ashdown run out, 2; G Holt b Tompson, 0; T Headle c Pedder b Robinson, 1; G. Fowler b Robinson, 8; A Sheome not out, 0; Extras, 11; total, 100. G. Tompkins and A Sturman did not bat.

DUNSTABLE SECOND ELEVEN v. MARKYATE STREET.—This return match was played on the Cheverells Park cricket ground, by kind permission of Mr. B. Bennett, on Saturday. Dunstable had choice of innings and batted first, Anderson and Elliott going to the wickets. Four runs only had been scored when play was interrupted by a heavy shower. After a while the game was re-commenced, and a good stand was made by the batsmen, the wickets being kept intact until 66 were scored, when Elliotts was dismissed, and Anderson followed three runs later. Giddings and Beasley were settling down for run getting, when play was again interrupted for five minutes by a shower, at the end of which the villagers refused to again take the field, urging as an excuse that there was no likelihood of dismissing the Dunstable team that day. Not even the promise to declare with 10 more runs added was sufficient to induce them to take the field, and the game was therefore left in the following position:—Dunstable—W F Elliott, c and b Lee 27; O G Anderson, b Lee 34; T Giddings, not out 6; J Beasley, not out 3; extras 7; (2 wkts) 76.

DUNSTABLE BOROUGH GAZETTE: WEDNESDAY 14 AUGUST 1895 - 4.

OUR CAPTAIN.

MR L. G. R. THRING.

If there is one man to whom, more than others, Bedfordshire cricket owes its revival, and we might almost say its modern birth, that man is the genial and popular Head-master of the Dunstable Grammar School, Mr L. G. R. Thring. When, in 1888, the subject of our sketch took up his residence in our midst, the term "cricket" was practically a misnomer for the game which was played in what has often been called the "sleepy county." Save for the amateurish expositions with which we were favoured on our village greens, and the bat little better exhibitions given by our town clubs, "ye manly olde game" had died a natural death. There was then no county cricket club, we could boast few really decent wielders of the pillow, and as a county we were far in the rear as regarded England's national game. But better times were coming. Such a brightened state of affairs was indebted to the indefatigable and energetic spirit whose life at that time became interwoven with ours, and not many months elapsed ere our humble Bedfordshire folk saw that a renaissance was dawning. Space will not permit us to follow minutely our worthy leader in his herculean task of reviving the all but dead pastime, suffice it to say that, mainly through his persistent efforts and untiring zeal, the game became once more established, the County Cricket Club was formed, and a county team of which from the first we have had cause to be proud, was put into the field.

Bearing these historical facts in mind, we came to the conclusion that a short biographical sketch of "our captain" would be of the greatest interest to our readers, and, fully intent on "button-holing" the ardent cricketer, we wended our way the other evening to the scene where he and such like spirits do congregate together. It was after a hard day's work behind the wickets, and when those tyrannical white-coated gentlemen had, to the relief of all parties concerned, reluctantly pocketed the balls, that we spotted our victim stalking in his manly, characteristic strides across the sodden turf. A neatly executed detour, and various winding oscillatory movements at last brought us into close quarters, and then, boldly running him to earth, with a decidedly menacing and aggressive air, we whipped out our note-book and pencil.

What a perfect horror some of our public favourites have of the interviewing fiend! And to what lengths will they not go in vain endeavours to avoid him? Thus it was with the subject of our sketch. Poor fellow! Had he but known half the uncanny means which Pressman may call to their aid in order to attain their ends, he would at once have assumed that docile, martyr-like expression, and submitted like a gentleman to the trying ordeal.

"Ah, yes! I know what you want," (with a ghastly attempt at a smile). "I first saw the light at Uppingham in 1862, and from there ——"
"Half-a-minute, please. You were living at Uppingham ——"
"With my father, the Rev J. C. Thring, who was then Head Master of the Uppingham Grammar School. We remained there until I was two years of age, when we left for Bradford in Wiltshire."
"And your early education?"
"My earliest education was derived from my father. At nine years of age I returned to the Uppingham School, where the next four years of my life were spent. I left there at the age of the fever, and at 13 I was sent to Marlborough."
"It was here, I believe, you first learned cricket?"
"Quite so. Of course I had previously handled both bat and ball, but it was at Marlborough that I was grounded in the game. I met with fairly good success, and at 17 I was in the eleven."
"When did you play your first county cricket?"
"In 1880. I was playing in the Wiltshire team, and we were pitted against South Wales. I believe in my first innings I knocked up 20, but in my second venture only four came to my credit."
"But that was pretty good for a colt. From Marlborough you went to Cambridge?"
"Yes, that year I went to Cambridge, but I did very little at cricket there."
We knew the truth. It was our victim's innate modesty which led him to seek to hide the truth from us. But those who know anything about L. G. R.'s brilliant career at Cambridge will easily guess that he had very little time to waste over leather-hunting. But we let him have his way, and assuming an innocent look, continued:
"And where after Cambridge?"
"After leaving Cambridge, I took a mastership at Wellingborough Grammar Schools. There I played a great deal of cricket. The masters' eleven was an exceptionally strong team. I went as master to Wellingborough in 1884, and scored more runs at Wellingborough than in any other period of my cricketing career."
"What was your top score there?"
"Well, as far as I can remember, it was when we were playing the Olney team. That day I scored 361."
"Ah, yes; and that is when you 'hit the top,' as uncle Jonathan says. But now I think we will come to scenes nearer home."
"As you please. I came here in 1888. Soon afterwards the County Club was formed, and I have been hon sec. and captain of the team from its start."

This was really exasperating. We had hoped to hear the "governor's" account of how he brought about that happy state of affairs, and here he was glossing it over without a word about himself. But our victim was already showing signs of uneasiness, and fearing lest we should prematurely lose our prey, we waived the point.
"Is it not a fact that you have been at the top of our county batting averages every year since the club's inauguration?"
"That is quite so. My best performance in county cricket was last year, when we were trying conclusions with the M.C.C. I went in first on that occasion, and played through the whole of the innings, my record at the end being 127 not out."
"I believe you once saw P. W. Maude give a brilliant display of batting?"
"Yes. I was playing for Wiltshire against the M.C.C. when Maude made a century in each innings against us. In that match I met with my greatest success as a bowler. Eight wickets fell to my share for 40 runs, and this out of a total of 200 up against us."
"Which do you consider your best county season?"
"Well, taken as a whole, I think last year was about the best I have had yet. At one time last season I had made 500 runs for once out. I have not had such good luck this year."
But the subject of our sketch was fast becoming unmanageable. Making a desperate fight to the, and we rapidly fired off our remaining questions:
"Height did you say? Well I am 5ft. 6in. I weigh about 11 stone, and really by nature I am not too inquisitive."
We took the gentle hint, and cordially thanking our genial captain for his courtesy, we departed.
A. W. M.

DUNSTABLE BOROUGH GAZETTE. WEDNESDAY 21 AUGUST 1895-3.

DUNSTABLE TOWN FIRST ELEVEN v.
ASTON CLINTON.

Played at Aston Clinton on Tuesday, and resulted in an easy win for the visitors by 148 runs. For the winners Manton and Anderson made a splendid stand for the 2nd wicket, putting on no fewer than 104 runs before being parted, when the former left for a grandly played 67, the other principal scores being Giddings 30, Staunton 32, Anderson 28, Pepper 24, and 30 standing to the credit of "Mr Extras." The home wickets fell very quickly before the bowling of W. F. Brown. Langley for the home team batted well for 25, and a stand of nearly an hour's duration was made by Chessall and Pitt, but only 20 runs were added to the score, the play during that time being very slow and monotonous. TOWN SECOND ELEVEN v. CADDINGTON.
Played at Dunstable on Thursday, and ended in a win for the home team by 9 runs on the first innings. The batting of the visitors was exceedingly feeble the only one who seemed able to play the bowling was Samm, who carried his bat for 11; he also took five of the home wickets. The runs for the winners were mainly from the bats of three men—Anderson 15, Love 12, and Marcham 16. In bowling Smith took seven for 18 runs, Gray two for 12. Score :—

TOWN SECOND ELEVEN v. CARTER'S C.C.
The town second eleven added one more to their long list of victories, on Saturday last, on the town ground, by defeating Carter's C.C. by 45 runs after a good game. The home batting was fairly good, the principal scores being: Love 21, Gray 21, Tompkins 14, Poulton 10, and Wright 13 (not out). On the visitors' side Hunker played well for 21, as also did Mooring and Timm for 11 and 10 respectively. In bowling Giddings for Carter's took six of the town wickets at a small cost, while the wickets of the visitors were equally divided among four of the home bowlers. Score,

DUNSTABLE VOLUNTEERS v. LUTON DETACHMENT.
This match was played at Luton on Saturday, and resulted in an easy win for Dunstable. Luton batted first, and were all disposed of for 19 runs owing to the very successful bowling of Corporal Smith. Dunstable scored 71, thus winning by 56 runs. The best scores for the winners were made by Corporal Smith and Private Hunker (21 each). The following comprise the Dunstable team:— Colour Sergt. Elliott, Sergt. R. Hambling, Sergt. W. D. Hambling, Corporal Smith, Private Hunker, Private Sharratt, Private Bonham, Private Burch, Private Darby.

READING OBSERVER: SATURDAY 25 MAY 1895-4

CRICKET.

IMPORTANT TO SECRETARIES.—As in former years, we readily grant a portion of our space for a local match reports, but we must impress upon secretaries the importance of sending in the scores as soon as possible after matches are played. We further desire to intimate that no score can be inserted unless it is written legibly and the innings set out in the form in which the matches are published. Sheets for drawing up reports can be obtained gratis on applying at the Observer Office. Notices of forthcoming matches will be inserted at the top of our cricket column at the rate of 1s. for each insertion. Match cards, posters, and bills of all kinds are printed with the utmost despatch and on strictly reasonable terms at the Observer Office.

The eleven has played 23 matches, won 11, lost 6,and drawn 5, having scored 4,363 for 237 wickets, an average of 10 25 runs per wicket, against 2,371 for 222 wickets, an average of 10·22 per wicket, by their opponents. Four times they scored over 200, six times over 150, and six times over 100, whilst their rivals never reached 200 only, five times exceeded 150,and six times exceeded 100. The highest score was 253 for 7 wickets, and the lowest 45. The highest score against them was 17s for 7 wickets, the lowest 25. R H Jackson has taken 14 catches at the wicket, while in the field H A Turner caught 10, A T Cliff and N W Parfitt 8,and F G Clarke and J Hodge 7.

(† Signifies not out.)
    Regarding the abilities of the players the following should prove of interest:—R. H. Jackson(1892-5) captain. Very useful behind the wicket, and is a smart catch ; as a bat is steady, correct, and reliable, but not powerful.—J. Hodge (1892-5). A much improved bowler, a brilliant field. A really good bat with a variety of strokes, but save for one excellent innings has been fairly reliable.—A. T Cliff (1892-5). Is an undying bowler and has done some good performances. Very sound field. Bats with great force, hits hard and clean.—R. H. Morris (1893-5). Has improved somewhat in the last few matches, but earlier he showed nothing of his old form.—F. G. Clarke (1894-5). Very steady and most useful bat. Has fielded brilliantly all through the season.—H. A. Turner (1894-5). Has not quite fulfilled the promise of last year ; has, however, improved, and at times batted well. A fair and improving field.—P. L. Housley (1894-5). Has abundantly justified expectations. Has a nice style and has played some excellent innings. A very clever cover-point.—F. Parfitt (1895). A painstaking bat with a nice off drive and a thoroughly reliable point. It promises of great promise.—W. Hatt (1895). A good left-handed bowler. Much improved in the field.—L. E. Todd (1895). A plucky bat, but lacks judgment. A good clean field when the ball comes straight to him, but does not start soon enough.—R. H. Mousley (1895). An erratic change bowler, very fair field and muscular bat.

BERKSHIRE CHRONICLE: SATURDAY 10 AUGUST 1895-2.

READING CRICKET WEEK.

A new departure in connection with the Reading Cricket Club has been inaugurated this year in the direction of a cricket week. The matches have been played during the last few days, and the cricket week has been a conspicuous success. Reading have been able to rely on excellent teams for each of their matches, such powerful exponents of the summer pastime as Capt. Nepean, Mr L. G. A. Collins, Mr H. J. Collins, Mr W. O. Nares, Mr F. E. Chapman, Rev C. E. Chapman, F. W. Finch, F. Doane, rendering assistance, &c., and some interesting cricket has been witnessed.

FIRST MATCH—v. MARYLEBONE C.C.

The week opened with this annual fixture against the M.C.C. The visitors had the assistance of four professionals, Handford, Pike, Sherwin, and Rylott, and two members of the Reading Club, Mr F. T. Baker and Mr Wm. Greenfield. Mr C. T. Murdoch, M.P., captained the visiting team.

Inclement weather greatly interfered with the game, and it had to be finally abandoned. Reading went in first to bat, and thanks mainly to A. Finch (35), H. J. Collins (25), H. G. A. Collins (27), succeeded in compiling 186 runs for nine wickets, when they declared their innings closed. M.C.C. then went in, but after scoring 15 for one wicket, stumps were drawn owing to the rain. Mrs Murdoch kindly entertained the teams at lunch and tea. In addition to Mr Murdoch, M.P., Mrs Murdoch and party, there were present Mrs Arthur, and Mrs and Miss Pott, Mr Stanley Pott and party, the Misses Finch, Mrs James Simonds, Miss Maurice, Mrs W. C. Brady, Mr Sidney Maurice, Mrs Hayward, Col. Taylor, &c. The following were the scores:—

SECOND MATCH—v. MR C. E. KEYSER'S XI.

Mr C. E. Keyser, of Aldermaston, who is an ardent cricketer, brought a strong team to Reading on Tuesday to play the local eleven. Mr Keyser's XI included C. E. Hiscock, of Newbury, A. Finch, S. H. Maurice, and W. M. Greenfield, of Reading. Reading were successful in winning the toss, and their innings was remarkable for the fine display of batting by Mr L. G. A. Collins and Capt. Nepean, who made 161 and 108 respectively. When the score reached 301 Reading declared their innings closed. Mr Keyser's XI began well, but the "rot" set in amongst the tail, and the innings closed for 105, Reading winning easily by 196 runs and four wickets. F. W. Finch took three wickets for 25 runs, Pott three for twenty, and Collins two for 4. The Reading Cricket Club provided lunch and tea for the teams. Among those present at the match were Major Mackenzie Edwards, Rev — Gilliam (Sonning), Colonel Taylor, Mr E. Sturges, Miss Garrard, Mr Glade Garrard, Mr J. H. Millard, the Misses Finch, Mrs James Simonds, and Miss Simonds. Scores:—

READING STANDARD: FRIDAY AUGUST 2 1895-2.

READING v. ABINGDON.—That the cricket field is one of the favourite haunts of King Chance was evidenced beyond doubt in this engagement, which was decided at Reading on Saturday. On the occasion of their first match, played a short time since at Abingdon, Reading were represented by a fairly powerful combination, nevertheless they were the recipients of a decisive thrashing. The Berks county town, however, created a mild surprise in the return engagement, as despite that their eleven on paper was particularly weak they returned the compliment they received at Abingdon with slight interest, inasmuch as they secured more than an innings victory. Our house captain used to his colleagues to the wicket first and before Crowhurst and Campion could be parted a partnership of 41 runs had been made. No serious advance in the figures was recorded before the promising G. Williams handled the willow, he going to third wicket down. Between the third and fourth dismissal 32 chalks were added, but after this stage no remarkable was chronicled, and when the umpires declared the homedayers was cherished, and when the umpires declared the homesters essay at a termination, 143 was the total. Williams with a score of 41 was the not out, the other chief contributors being Crowhurst (17), Campion (14), Pott (10), and A. H. Hynott (13), each of whom provided a good exhibition. On the visitors featuring, it seemed to Campion and Hynott, it was not difficult to forecast that their fate was sealed after but a few minutes play. Dandridge, who is generally looked upon a safe bat, was caught when one run only had been compiled, and when three wickets were down three chalks was the grand total. Lovett scored fourteen, but in doing so had many fresh partners, as might be gathered from the remark that at the fall of fourth wicket the total was 12; 5th, 14; six, 10; seven, 30; eight, 30 nine, 35; ten, 36. Following on, the visitors started in healthy style as the first wicket realised 25. After this, however, the rot noticeably set in, and the reason was apparent, as the board showed that in their second innings only 63 were compiled. Of this number Dandridge totaled 8 and A. B. Morland 8. So splendidly did both Campion and H. Hynott trundle, that the home captain was content to trust the whole of the slow trundling department. That his judgment was sound was demonstrated by the capital second achievement of each, which produced: Campion in the first innings bowled 48 balls, and scored one maiden and six wickets at a cost of twenty runs, and in the second delivered 63 balls, and again scored six wickets for 34 runs. Hynott's analysis read: First innings, nine overs, three maidens, 13 runs four wickets—second 13-12. Twelve overs, three maidens, 33 runs, four wickets. Score:—

GUILDHALL C.C. v NEWTOWN C.C.— These clubs met on Saturday in their return fixture at Sandleford park. The game, which ended in a win for Guildhall, was remarkable for low scoring, the bowlers taking everything before them, in addition to which the fielding at both sides was good. Humphries and Lucas for Guildhall bowled splendidly, the former capturing 12 wickets for as many runs, and the latter 7 for 3. Allsop and Williamson took the bowling honours for Newtown. PARISH CHURCH INSTITUTE v HIGHCLERE.— The return match between these teams was played, by kind permission, on the Institute ground on Thursday, in splendid weather. Highclere winning the toss decided to bat first, but owing to the bowling of Gunning and Gayfer, the first four wickets fell for 21, when Collins came in and made a useful stand, necessitating several changes in bowling. Several runs came, Collins doing the lion's share, but Gunning going in at the finish finely beat him with a beauty, whilst the Thirkills made some useful stands. After the interval the Institute quickly disposed of Pyke, the former hitting his wicket, whilst Pyke contented himself by keeping up his wicket. Maslin made the next most useful stand, lively putting on 18 in quick time. The others making decent figures were Head, Storer and Gunning. The teams were entertained to tea, through the kindness of Miss L. Golding and Mr H Golding. Score:—

SPEEN v. BURGHCLERE.—This match was played at Speen on Saturday. Speen, winning the toss, sent Ashby and Freebody to the wickets, the former being uncomfortably bowled, the ball hitting him on the chest and rebounding on to the wickets. A Smith then joined Freebody and a long stand was made Speen putting on 148 before the innings was declared closed. Both Smith and Freebody played good cricket for their respective scores, 71 and 64. Burghclere then went in and totalled 63 before being dismissed. Sherman and Storey did not bat. E body was most successful with the ball, obtaining five wickets. NEWBURY v. 49TH REGIMENTAL DISTRICT.—The return match between these two elevens was played on Wednesday on the ground of the former in perfect order. The soldiers, who had got together a strong team, were the first to bat and did well, the batting of E. O. Wells and the bowling of H. D. Wells and S. Coley were most successful. Newbury then batted and did well. Two batsmen were separated. We read the wickets went down. The end was top notch. The last two batsmen were quickly dismissed, the whole side being all out before lunch for 149. Pte Bounds, who showed the form, was not out 59, his best hits being three fours, five threes, and ten twos, the only other batsman to make a stand were Bromley (23) and Croome (31). Going in a second time, the soldiers were dismissed for a smaller score. H. T. Hill and Cave opposing the bowling of Pudding and Newbury and getting wickets. Unfit to decide who, the innings of both stopped in that afternoon secured the professional Newbury team. We watched both sides as they played good cricket. H. D. Wells went in and Hill was directly dismissed for the first. Both sides tried their strength combined and the two monopolies mastered the bowling, the result of which was very good. One or two passes before Are a such. Wells still continued to bat before the match was over. The soldiers having joined, and they failed to bring about the desired effect. Meeting and the visitors were compelled to defer to 153 for owing to leaving by the 9 train, the score standing at 103 for 6 wickets, and a draw was declared. Both teams helped to gave a fine display of batting. They were at the wickets first with the next score total for 6 wickets at per se 115 runs. ABINGDON HERALD: SATURDAY 7 SEPTEMBER 1895

ABINGDON CRICKET AND FOOTBALL CLUB.

CRICKET SEASON, 1895.

Matches arranged: 21; played, 17; won, 8; lost, 7; drawn, 2; scratched, 4. The averages of the 1st XI. in both batting and bowling are appended:
It will be noticed that the late captain, Mr. F. D. Curtis, heads the batting 1st with an average of 24.50, Payne and Wilson coming next with 16.33 and 16.25 respectively, while Dandridge, Allin, W. Legge, F. H. Fryce and Skurray all get into double figures. Messrs. A. C. M. Croome and F. Deane appeared once each, and scored over the century, 117 being Mr. Croome's score and 109 Mr. Deane's. In the bowling Payne, Dandridge and H. S. Challenor have borne the brunt, the former taking 27 wickets at an average cost of 5.37 runs, Dandridge 49 at an average of 7.97 runs, and Challenor 37 wickets at an average of 9.02; all very good performances. Messrs. W. T. Morland, Wilson, Lovett, Legge and Ward have all rendered useful help in this department. Though the 1st XI. have achieved successes over Oxford City and Reading, they have not done so well generally, as in other years. The worst feature of this year's proceedings is the number of matches scratched. This we consider a bad omen for the club, as the reason given for the scratchings is the inability of the officials to raise teams. This ought not to be with any club having so many playing members on the books. Each member is supplied with a fixture-list at the beginning of the season, and as the success of the club should be the first thought of all members we greatly wonder at so many members engaging to play other matches on dates fixed for the club matches, but to this can be traced the falling off in the 1st XI. performances. If all the subscribing members would pull together, the Abingdon Cricket and Football Club would never lack a team, either 1st or 2nd, that would fail to uphold the honour of the club and the town which supports it, and we hope in future there will be no difficulty in this particular direction. We heartily congratulate the club on the success it has so far achieved and the members may be assured that if the remedies prescribed are properly applied there is a brilliant future in store.

R. Ely in three overs took two wickets for five runs, and T. Skurray (two overs) one wicket for eight runs.

THE SECOND ELEVEN
although not so successful this year as last, have been able to win more matches than they have lost, and in three cases out of five, had time permitted the drawn games to be played out the gains would most probably have been increased. The batting averages show a slight falling off. Jackson, who last year had an average of 22, takes second place with 15, but Glanville and Steptoe show marked improvement. Heath carries off the honours, but he has only played in six innings. There are several young players who claim attention, and it would be well for older members next season to look to these rising batsmen if they wish the club to prosper. Matcham, who only played three innings, was not out on each occasion and winds up with an average of 58. In the bowling department a word of commendation is due to Glanville, who has bowled admirably. Heath and Fryce have both bowled well. If a great deal of the net practice was abolished and scratch teams were got together to practice, the fielding would be better. An improvement in that respect is much needed, for if the fielding had been smarter several matches could have been pulled out of the fire this season. Matches played, 12; won, 4; lost, 3; drawn, 5.

NEWBURY WEEKLY NEWS: THURSDAY 12 SEPTEMBER 1895-1.

NEWBURY CRICKET CLUB, 1895.

Thirty matches were arranged, of which 22 only were played; eight were scratched (six by opponents two by Newbury). The General Election caused Newbury to scratch their two. Of the matches played 15 were won, 4 were lost, and 3 drawn. A record was established by Newbury winning their last six matches consecutively, which included every match (five in number) in the cricket week. Mr. C. E. Slocock's 50 wickets for 823 runs per wicket was a very fine performance. In the six last matches (including the cricket week ones) the players were all bona-fide members of the Newbury Cricket Club, and with very few exceptions were local men. The captain (Mr. J. L. Flin) is entitled to a word of praise. It is no doubt greatly due to his tact and knowledge of the members and of their capabilities, that the club did so well. The teams have, so far as one can judge, never done otherwise than played together in a thoroughly good and sportsmanlike spirit, the result showing that 'unity is strength.'

NEWBURY WEEKLY NEWS. THURSDAY 12 SEPTEMBER 1895 - 2

CRICKET.

Mr. SHEPHERD'S XI. beat WOOLLEY PARK.
This match was played at Woolley Park on Wednesday. Woolley is first going in made over the total of 67, of which Hayden and Smith did best, with 13 and 14 respectively. Mr. Shepherd's team then went in, the score at lunch time standing at 81 for 5 wickets, Hutchins and Weaver playing good cricket for their side. The innings closed for 97, Woolley going in again put on 66. Mr. Shepherd's team now required 37 runs to win, which they did for the loss of 3 wickets, Hutchins again doing well. Both teams were kindly entertained to tea after the match by Mr. Winchester. Score:

INKPEN beat FALKLAND C.C.
The visitors to meet the Inkpen C.C. on their own ground were rather late in coming, the time fixed for the match being two o'clock, which did not let play commence till about four. The home team won the toss and began play quickly. The Falkland score was looked rather small at one time, four wickets having fallen for 30 runs. It more soon changed for better, a thing of a splendid drive by A. A. two of a series of run nice singles by the Rev. H. Lewis who made good hits, and when the innings was closed carried run his bat. R. Butler and J. Abraham did not bat. The visitors were dismissed for 29 runs, mainly owing to the first-rate bowling of T. May, who took 7 wickets, his average being 3. Score :-

STOCKCROSS beat CHIEVELEY.
In lovely weather this the return match, was played at Stockcross on Saturday last, and after a very pleasant game was won by the home team by 68 runs. Chieveley, going to the wickets first, were all disposed of for 79 runs; Leach, Smith, and Wright being top scorers, whilst the bowling honours were shared by Messrs. J. Sturgess, J. Wicks, and E. Smith, the last named taking three wickets for one run. Stockcross on going in managed to put together 147 runs, J. Freebody being top scorer with 38, and Smith and J. Calkin making 8 runs each, J. LAMBOURN beat CHILTON.
The return match was played on Saturday last, on the Lambourn ground, and, as the score will show, resulted in a win for Lambourn, the home-men thus avenging their defeat in the previous match. The game did not commence until after four, consequently had to be decided on the first innings. The weather was delightful and the wicket really good. Chilton won the toss and elected to send the home team in. A very poor start was made, as two Lambourn wickets went down for 0, but a somewhat different complexion was put on the game, when, after 21, Padfield had scored 2-run in good style, F. Harris

and J. Bryant not well set. F. Harris made some splendid hits, and the spectators loudly cheered his plucky play, his score of 35 being made in a very short time. J. Mathews played capital cricket for his 9, and was greatly applauded upon his retirement. V. Hobbe and C. J. Mathews and others bowled gave a capital score, before the innings was declared closed at 119. The Chilton team went but little, only scoring 43. Particularly obtaining double figures. The bowling of Messrs. Hobbs and Bryant was very effective, and F. Harris made two capital catches in the out-field. Chilton followed on, and at the call of time no wicket had fallen for 41 runs, Barnes, Rumboll having scored well during the innings. Score :-

SOUTHINGTON beat MA. A. KINGSMILL'S XII.
This highly exciting and closely contested match was played in lovely weather on the charmingly situated Sydmonton Cricket ground, by partial counts, who are near boundaries to it, several, however, had many years still before them for they reach this degree of experience. We would draw attention to the excellent pavilion on the Southington site of Kneller and Pymter, and the notice of Mr. Wyndham Portal's North. The star of the Sydmonton team was J. Chute whose bowling was as successful as his batting, and his prowess was awarded by the presentation but offered for the highest score by Mr. Wyndham Portal, Leyland and Harrison had distinguished themselves in taking Southington wickets, aided by the smart wicket-keeping of Andrew Macdonald, in spite of their strenuous exertions Southington proved successful by the narrow margin of seven runs. BUSHCROSS beat NEWTOWN.
Played on the Sandl ford Priory ground and ended in a defeat for the visitors. Visitors won the toss and batted first; three wickets fell quickly, but on Rev. O. litton and G. Kimber getting together runs were piled on fast, Kimber being run out for a well-played 17. Rev. H. litton carrying out his bat for a brilliantly-his innings of 56. Newtown made no stand whatever, G. Aldus putting for 41 and obtaining double figures. This match brought the Newtown season to a close, after a fairly successful season, they having won five matches and lost four, with eight. After a very disasterous start they pulled up in made last fixtures, winning three out of their last five. Score :-

NEWTOWN beat PARISH CHURCH INSTITUTE.
This return match was played in the beautiful grounds of Sandleford Park, on Saturday, August 31st, before a very large number of people and players, including Lady Butler and family and company. Newtown went first to the wickets, and by good play and hard hitting on the part of Elfield (22), and Gardiner (12), made a total of 48 runs. Good bowling by the Institute (A. W.) restricted, and being well backed up by Matthews and Newtonm). On the Institute standing up to the wickets, Newtown were soon put up for 30 runs. Vickers being top scorer with 10. Gibbs, who had twice been overlooked batting the first. Tea was then partaken of, and Newtown again went to the wickets, but the Institute not being able to go in again, the match ended on a pleasant game on the first innings by eight runs.

ADBURY HOUSE beat NEWTOWN HOUSE.
On Thursday afternoon a match was played between elevens of the Adbury House and Newtown House, the same being won by the Adbury House eleven, a result being as truly due to the good bowling of B. and G. Cooper, mainly. The fielding of G. Cooper brought up a beautiful catch saviorly, and C. Berry made many thanks for 38. Among the bowlers, Stratford, White, Street, and Weight acquitted themselves very well. A capital tea was presented to the housekeeper on the grounds, which the players did justice too. Altogether a most enjoyable afternoon was spent, and the hearty thanks were accorded with acclaim to Mr. Walter Arkwright Jr. his kindness. Score :-

CRICKET.

HECKFIELD v. MAJOR MILDMAY'S XI.—Played at Highfield Park, Heckfield on September 10, and resulted in an easy victory for the home team, a fact mainly due to an excellent innings by Bye, marred only by a single chance. For the visitors, Major Mildmay played sound cricket for twenty-seven. A large and fashionable gathering watched the game with much interest. Score:
Heckfield: Rev H. Salter, 6—37; E. Barnes, 8—5; I. Hradfield, 5—12; J. Bye, 59 (not out)—10; Rev G. S. Davies, 0—2; F. H. Martin, 1—2; P. F. Law, 8—4 (not out); C. A. Law, 0—5 (not out); W. H. P. Law, 0; A. Dodd, 24—18; Dr Comber, 2; extras, 4—1. Totals, 117 and 101 (for seven wickets).
Major Mildmay's XI: E. Beagley, 5; C. Armiger, 0; L. Warren, 6; Major Mildmay, 27; P. Mildmay, 2; Rev J. Saundersou, 0; G. Mildmay, 0; W. G. Cope, 8; M. L. Cope, 0; A. White, 4; J. Pavey (not out), 6; extras, 7. Total, 59.

FARINGDON ADVERTISER: SATURDAY 2 NOVEMBER 1895.

CRICKET CLUB—The annual dinner of the Lechlade Cricket Club, was held at the New Inn Hotel, on Thursday evening last. About 24 members and friends sat down to the very excellent repast provided by Mrs Richens, to which ample justice was done. The proceedings were presided over by Mr H. W. Prior-Wandesford, who was supported by Captain Tosswill, Dr. J. O. March, Mr W. Crosland, the vice-chair being occupied by Mr H. Milward. It being the first time the president had been present at the dinner, he most generously provided after dinner a very old-fashioned but acceptable drink viz., "rum punch," with which the toasts were drunk. The following was the toast list. "The Queen," by the chairman, "The President," by Mr H. Milward, responded to by the chairman, "Lechlade Cricket Club," by the chairman, responded to by Mr S. Campbell and Mr G. A. Davis, who, as is usual, read the averages for batting and bowling during the season, "Donor of Prizes," by Mr Davis, responded to by Mr H. Milward and Mr S. Campbell, "Tradesmen of Lechlade," by Captain Tosswill, responded to by Mr F. Stroud, "The Visitors," by Mr Crosland, responded to by Dr. J. O. March, "The Ladies," by Mr T. Slatter, responded to by Mr E. Bowley, "Mrs Richens," by Mr B. F. Innocent, responded to by Mr Richens, "Vice-President" (Mr H. Milward), by the chairman, "Captain," by Mr Davis, responded to by Mr B. F. Innocent. During the evening, Mr Campbell and Mr Milward presented the bat and ball, which was won by Mr W. Johns, to his father, Mr J. Johns, to be forwarded. Some capital songs were sung by the following—Captain Tosswill, Mr W. Crosland, Messrs. B. E. Innocent, Gibbs, Hudson, and Davis.

MAIDENHEAD ADVERTISER: WEDNESDAY 13 NOVEMBER 1895.

MAIDENHEAD CRICKET CLUB.

THE BATTING AND BOWLING AVERAGES.

We have received the batting and bowling averages of the above club. The first eleven have played 20 matches, of which ten were won, four drawn and six lost. The club scored 2,728 runs for 204 wickets, an average of 13·37 per wicket, whilst their opponents scored 2,024 runs for 221 wickets, an average of 11·87 per wicket. BRECON AND RADNOR COUNTY TIMES: FRIDAY 30 AUGUST 1895 - 2.

LOCAL SPORT.
CRICKET.--BRECON v. LLANDOVERY.

This match was played at Llandovery on Wednesday the 21st inst. Brecon batted first and made a poor show, being all dismissed for 49. D. T. M. Jones took 6 wickets for 29. Llandovery were dismissed for the comparatively small total of 95, W. Ll. Thomas taking most of the wickets. Brecon fared little better in their second innings, being only able to compile 52. This left Llandovery 7 to win, and they lost 3 wickets before they got that number. The wicket was in a very bad condition and played badly. Scores:—

The Wycombe Y.M.G.A. C.C. play Newland Bible Class on the Rye to-morrow (Saturday). Wickets pitched at 2.30.
Wycombe First XI. have no fixture for to-morrow, but the Reserves go to Marlow to play Berkese School (return). On Thursday—not Wednesday as at first arranged—the Grammar School Past and Present play the Wycombe Club, on which occasion we hear that Mr. and Mrs. Pashell have kindly offered to provide luncheon and tea for the teams. This match ought to prove a good even game, as several of the Club's First XI. are old boys and will play for the School. We also hear that on Saturday week, when the final in the Nesting Cup Competition takes place, the Vicar and Mrs. Shaw intend inviting all the members of the Club to supper, and that Mr. Shaw has consented to present the Cup and medals at the conclusion of the game, but we shall be able next week to give fuller details of the arrangements for the day.
St. John's C.C. play the West End United on the Recreation Ground to-morrow at 2.30.—St. John's C.C. play matches for the Saturdays in August.—R. Ford, 5, West End-road, Wycombe.
Church Room C.C. play Wheeler End Blue Star, at Wheeler End, to-morrow, at 2.30.

SOUTH BUCKS FREE PRESS: FRIDAY 9 AUGUST 1895-3.

HIGH WYCOMBE v. MR. E. STEVENS' XI.
On Saturday Mr. Edmund Stevens, of The Vache, Chalfont St. Giles, brought over a strong team to oppose the local club, but rain prevented the game from being completed. It was, however, drawn in favour of Wycombe, who had made 49 for the loss of two wickets at the time of drawing. How, it was possible before luncheon, and a start was not made till past 3 o'clock, when the visitors went to the wicket. Before their innings was completed, a smart shower compelled them to make a precipitate rush to the friendly shelter of the pavilion. The rain holding off for a time allowed the innings to be finished, 57 runs being the result. On Wycombe going in the light was very bad, and the rain came down at intervals in torrents, so that after several attempts to play out time it was thought best to leave the game as a draw. The state of the match was left in the position shown by the following score and analysis. The wet did not hinder Haines from making some good strokes, his 29 including a 4, two 4's, three 3's, and two 2's:—

BAYNERS XI v. PERMANENT STAFF OF THE 2nd BATT. OXFORD LIGHT INFANTRY.
On Monday last, August 5th, a match was played on Stokyon private ground, between the above teams, and, after being hindered a little by the unsettled weather, ended in a decisive victory for the former by an innings and 63 runs. Mr. O. M. Rose captained the home eleven, and, having won the toss, elected to bat. The game was very slow at the commencement, and 4 good wickets were lost for 21, but when Fountaine and Mr. Powell resumed associated, the best stand of the day was made, and some good cricket was seen, both playing with confidence. They put on 62 runs between them, when Mr. Rose was caught at the wicket for an invaluable 27. Powell left soon after with a well-played 39 to his credit. Wheeler made 14, and the remainder being speedily disposed of, the innings closed for 114. An adjournment was now made for lunch, which had been prepared (in a spacious marquee, erected for the occasion) through the generosity of Sir Philip and Lady Rose. After ample justice had been done to the good things provided, the visitors went in for their innings. Captain Rose and Bugler Wakehin were the only ones to offer any resistance to the attacks of O. M. Rose and Powell, who were bowling in their best form, and the innings closed for 51. Captain Rose 13 was the best of the batting display, which included one 6, a clean drive out of the ground. Powell had the fine analysis of 6 wickets for 23, while Mr. Rose's cost 23 runs. Having to follow on, they failed to reach the number required to save an innings' defeat, Dean taking 6 wickets for 11 runs, and the game resulted as before stated. Full score and analysis:—

BURNHAM v. FLACKWELL HEATH.
This match was down for decision at Burnham on Saturday. The home team winning the toss, played out. Burnham winning the toss, Tilbury put his men in first; and though a start could not be made before 4 o'clock, they knocked up 127 runs for the loss of 5 wickets when play ceased. MARLOW v. ORIEL.
Played at Marlow on Monday in very showery weather. The local club found it difficult to raise a side, and the teams were only eleven a side, but the innings were decidedly short. The team which took the field included H. H. Meade, who is playing at Marlow, the season being his weak point. Marlow won the toss and batted. Meade, Langley, and Thurlow were the most prominent men. The Marlow innings was made by Bover and Borton, who carried the score from 112 for eight wickets to 175 for nine. Barton left a score of 50 and Friend 74 out of 99. The innings closed for 175. The visitors made an excellent start with Arundel and Coleman, both scoring steadily, and when each had hit Meade and Coleman, and 17 went up before Baillie effected a separation and their partnership was worth 128 runs. After-wards the Oriel men did badly. Arundel was out just at 96, but scored 96 with a score of near on 170, and later 6, died 44, etc. Lansley took up the bowling with the total at 107 for four wickets and met with great success, securing five wickets for 17 runs, in rather steady bowling. The innings closed for 124, and Marlow therefore won by 51 runs. Score:—

South Bucks Free Press: Friday 9 August 1895-4.

Cricket.
(Other news on page 6).

WYCOMBE v. ORIEL.
A team from which Fred Langley, Bird, Baines, Mumford, Raffety, and the R.v. K.D. Shaw were absentees did not look on paper strong enough to do battle successfully for Wycombe when they had to meet the Oriel club on Tuesday. However, with the assistance of G. Langley and G. Langley—the latter playing for his brother—and W. Stone, a Wycombe man, who, since his residence in the Midlands, has been tried in the Warwickshire Colts' team, they managed to win on the first innings by 47 runs, to which Keen contributed 33 and Stone 25 and four other batsmen also got into double figures, so that when the last wicket fell the total had reached 151. Unfortunately Page was unable to bowl, having twisted his knee in turning to make his last run; but Keen and G. Langley bowled with such effect that Reid and W.H. Percival were the only two among the visitors who made anything approaching a stand against their deliveries, and the whole side were disposed of for 54. The bowling of the Oriel men in Wycombe's second innings was somewhat loose, and got severely punished, as shown by the subjoined score and analysis:—

Y.M.C.A. play St. Anne's C.C. on the Rye next Saturday, at 2.30.

WYCOMBE MARSH F.C.—A general meeting of the above was held on Tuesday, July 30, to consider if they should enter the Junior League, when after some discussion it was proposed and carried. It was decided to adjourn the meeting until August 13th, at the Red Lion at 8 o'clock. All members are requested to attend, as the business will be election of officers, &c., for the next season.

CHALFONT PARK v. ST. SILAS (LONDON).—The annual match was played between the above, at Chalfont Park, on the 6th, and resulted in a win for the home team, who are to be congratulated, considering that whether they did not contend with (21). Sidney Golding played excellent cricket, 8 not out in the first and 24 in the second innings. Our bowled captain, taking 16 wickets for 13 runs. The visitors' scoring was very low; Austin bowled fairly well for them. Chalfont made 54 in the first innings and 131 for six wickets in the second. The visitors' twenty-one were all got rid of for 32.

BURNHAM v. POSTAL TELEGRAPHS (LONDON).—This match was played at Burnham on Monday last, when the visitors won a game, carried over from a previous week, easily. Burnham batted first, but were all out for 49—Tilbury 11, Baxter 16 (not out), and Palmer 10. James bowled splendidly, taking 6 for 20 runs, Rooks 2 for 14, and Miller 1 for 10. The Postal team fared better than their opponents, knocking up 65, W. Miller (13) and Bordeaux (26) debut. Baldwin took 6 for 14 and Baxter 4 for 16. Going in again, Burnham scored 61 for the loss of 4 wickets and then declared; Baker (26 not out), Woodley (16), Baldwin (14), and Wells (13 not out) being in form. The visitors then stopped further play, and the Telegraph Staff thus won by 12 runs on the first innings. Maule captured 3 wickets for 16 runs.

TAPLOW STATION v. BEACONSFIELD-GUARDS.—Played on the Station Ground on Saturday, the home team winning easily. After disposing of the visitors for 23 (T. Smith 12), Taplow replied 59 for 6 wickets when the game was stopped by rain. H. Palmer made 18, G. Jones 10 not out, and G. Frost 10. For the winners Fisher obtained 7 wickets for 20 runs, Frost 2 for 1, and J. Wright 1 for 2. In the second innings Rowland (3 for 13) and Garrett (1 for 10) were the successful trundlers.

GERRANDS CROSS v. GERRANDS CROSS.—This popular match was played on the charming ground at Evelyn, showery weather somewhat interfered with the game and only one innings each was played. The visitors scored 56; Gerrards Cross put together 113 and won by 49 runs. Captain Fenton played a most brilliant innings, scoring 48. Mrs. Worsley's hospitality to the players was much appreciated.

SOUTH BUCKS FREE PRESS: FRIDAY 16 AUGUST 1895 - 4

FLACKWELL HEATH 2ND XI. v. LITTLE MARLOW.
LOW SCORING.
A match between the above clubs was played on the ground of the latter on Saturday last, and resulted in a victory for Flackwell Heath by 25 runs on the first innings. The batting of Musk, Baker, and Smith was very good. Almond accomplished the hat trick, taking 3 wickets in 3 as twelve balls. Clifford also bowled well. The second innings of the latter proved most disastrous, they being dismissed in 20 minutes for 9 runs. No doubt the result would have been different had time permitted a second innings for the visitors. Davis and Penny batted in good form. In the second innings of Flackwell Heath Almond again accomplished the hat trick, and took 6 wickets for 1 run. Penny obtained 4 for 6 runs.

MARLOW v. HIGH WYCOMBE.
BIG HITTING BY H. H. MASSIE.
The return match between these old rivals was played at Marlow, on Saturday, in fine weather, on a wicket that favoured the bowlers. It will be remembered that the first match at Wycombe was left drawn much in favour of Marlow. On this occasion the riverside town gained an easy victory, thanks to a fine innings by H. H. Massie, the Australian batsman (who is staying at Marlow this summer), and some excellent bowling by W. H. Lunnon and Barber. Marlow won the toss, Langley and Barber facing the bowling of Page and H. S. Parker. None came steadily until the total reached 28, when both batsmen were out with a dozen runs to credit. Massie and Lunnon advanced the total to 48, when the latter was caught by White, and as Halnes failed to score, then came to the wicket Massie, and a fearful stand was effected. The 110 went up amidst great cheers, but four sending back Brown, Orton, Mackenzie was over. Six, seven, and eight for 104. Massie was now hitting with tremendous force, and it was especially scoring on Page, a gigantic hit for 6 into Court Garden delighting the onlookers hugely. Morgan batted steadily while the Australian hit until the total was 140, when he was yorked by Parker. Just when it appeared certain that Massie would take out his bat he was captured in the slips, and the innings closed for 146. Massie's dashing score of 80 included two 6's, eight 4's, two 3's, and eight 2's. He was deservedly applauded on retiring to the pavilion. Both Parker and Keen bowled well. Wycombe commenced their innings with F. M. Parker and Langley. The bowlers were W. H. Lunnon and Barber. A more disastrous start could not well be imagined. Lunnon caught and bowled Parker and clean bowled Halnes in his first over. Two by Gifford, George scored a couple of singles, and Langley was then caught by Mackenzie off Barber. Things for H. S. Parker helped George to put a better aspect upon affairs, but the former was out at 23, after hitting up 16. Jane followed in and was bowled by Lunnon at 25. The brothers George then got together and carried the score to 40, when three wickets fell—the George brothers and White, all clean bowled. Page hit up 14 by means of a 6, a 4, and two 2's, and the innings closed for 60. Lunnon bowled uncommonly well, his seven wickets costing 61 runs each. Barber followed on, and made 6 0 for one wicket. Langley batted well for 35 not out. Marlow therefore won by 68 runs on the first innings—a rather unexpected result, seeing that the team which was in the field was by no means a strong one. Score and analysis:—

"FINE APPLE" v. "KING'S HEAD."
This match was played on Monday, August 5th, in a meadow at Hard-in-hand Lady, lent by Mr. A. Gibbons, and resulted in a win for the "King's-head" by six runs and plus wickets. For the winners, Planridge did well, and Cross played well, and the fielding of the veteran G. Cross, was also good. Score:—

COLESHILL G.C. v. ST. MARGARET'S G.C.
The return match between the above clubs was played at Coleshill on Saturday, August 10th, St. Margaret's again proving successful, and due chiefly, it is said, to the brilliant all-round cricket of Mr. O. O. Powell, who not only made the highest score for his side, but actually took 8 wickets for 8 runs. Mr. J. Dean also bowled wonderfully well, taking the other 4 for 30 runs. Coleshill went to the wickets first, and were soon disposed of, Mr. E. M. Weatherby being top scorer with 11, and the innings closed for 23. St. Margaret's then commenced their task of making 20 to win, which was soon accomplished. Powell made 11, and Dean 5, a total of 23 which was compiled without the slightest hitch, and the innings reached 75. Coleshill went to the wickets a second time, and did much better, having made 72 for 8 wickets when time was called. Full score and analysis:—

MR. W. PEARCE'S (WYCOMBE) XI. v. SOUTHALL.
On Saturday last Mr. W. Pearce, of the "Nag's Head," London-road, captained an eleven to visit Southall to try conclusions with an eleven representing the Southall Gas Works. Although the Bucks men made a fine innings, having 113 runs to their credit, the ball were far too good for the home team, and for the loss of only 3 wickets they had contributed 157 runs. Cooper (76) was adjudged to be classed as belonging to the Middlesex team of Collins, and the way in which Dixon contributed his 76 showed that he was a batsman of no mean order. He hit the included one 5, three 4's, two 3's, and five 2's. William Cooper, too, knocked up eight successive hits in succession. In fact, against such formidable opponents the bowling of the Wycombe representatives was taxed to its utmost; but, nevertheless, a pleasant game was the result. Both W. Pearce and O. W. Harper showed good form for the losers. Score and analysis:—

GREAT MISSENDEN v. LEE COMMON.
The return match was played at Great Missenden on Thursday, August 22. The visitors went in first, starting with F. Lewis. Both sided played well, the former successfully defending his wicket for the whole of the innings and carrying his bat out. The component e's (sic) were 4, 3, and seven 5's and singles. H. Ayres made a useful 14. For the home eleven W. Attkins was particularly smart, caught after making 14. Dr. Turner played good cricket for the visitors and carried out his bat. Score:—

MR. COLLINS CLASS v. BOOKER TEMPERANCE.
The return match between these clubs took place at Booker on Saturday last, and a pleasant game resulted in a win for the Class by 20 odd runs. It is worthy of note that meeting of these teams has usually been a close game; last year the result was 42 to 37 in Class's favour, and this year the result of the fixtures has been the same. Batting for the Class made 56, Cox (33) and Collins (10) being chief contributors. Booker went in and their innings reached 42 when the last wicket fell, out of which number Burnham had made 16. In their second innings the Class only accounted for 27, neither batsman being able to make a stand against the Temperance bowling. In the end Booker (18) was the only one to get into double figures. MARRIED v. SINGLE.
On Tuesday a second match was played upon the same ground between Eleven Married and Eleven Single, Mr Hoare himself taking the bat with the Married Eleven. It was a well contested game, the Single team being the victors by 5 runs only. C Baker was successful with both ball and bat. Refreshments were dispensed to players and spectators, and in the evening a substantial supper was provided for all that had taken part in the game. About 30 sat down, and Mr Hoare joined the company. The tables being cleared, Mr Blake, in appropriate words, proposed the health of the host, which was drunk with musical honours. A social and harmonious evening was spent, such as Sutton cricketers have not often the opportunity of spending. Score :—

CAMBRIDGE EXPRESS: SATURDAY 3 AUGUST 1895-3

SPORT AND PLAY.

GOSSIP UPON CURRENT SPORTS AND PASTIMES.

I am vain enough to think that some of my readers have missed "Vim's" notes from the columns of the Express during the last fortnight. To them I offer an apology for my non-appearance in print. Like the majority of people, I caught the election fever, and, becoming absorbed in the progress of the fight, had no time to expend upon such mundane things as athletic notes. I am afraid I have rather got out of touch with the progress of events in the athletic world during the last fortnight, but will do my best to make up lost ground.

* *

My first duty is to express both pleasure and regret at the doings of the County Cricket Club. The drubbing the County team received from the M.C.C. and Ground was a severe one, although the Club team was not a particularly strong one. These curious collapses of our County teams are unexplainable by me, although those who are behind the scenes may be able to give a reason. With a somewhat stronger eleven, the County gave Hunts. on Wednesday a ditto to the treatment they had received from the M.C.C., viz., a single innings defeat. The record for the season thus far is one win and one loss. On Monday and Tuesday next, the County will entertain the M.C.C. and Ground at Cambridge, and it is to be hoped that they may be able to give a better account of themselves then.

The old suggestion has been revived in the select circle of enthusiasts of the game of the desirability of playing at least one County match each season upon Parker's Piece. The Hunts. match was peculiarly suited to such treatment, and it is a pity that it was not played on the public ground, and used to excite a greater interest amongst Cambridge people in the doings of the Club, the future of which looks none too rosy at present.

* *

The final of the Cambridgeshire Cricket Cup Competition will again be fought out by the Old Higher Grade, last year's winners, and the Camben. The old boys are such good sportsmen that one would like to see them win again, but, on the other hand, the Camben have been in the competition since the very commencement, and have had a great run of ill-luck, and their win would be a popular one. The final promises once more to be an extremely interesting one.

* *

The scratching of the Cassandra Club in this Competition has caused a good deal of public comment. I have had a conversation with the secretary, who declines to state why the Club scratched, although he affirms that they had good and sufficient reason for so doing, which I do not doubt. After all, action of this kind is the Club's private business, but it seems a pity the Cassandra should have knocked out the old Perseans, who would have gone through, and then have retired without vouchsafing any reason for so doing.

* *

I am really sorry for Sawston, who were so crushingly defeated by the Old Higher Grade last Saturday. What could have come over the Institute team? The whole side was disposed of for 11 runs, six of these being extras, and the only man who scored off the bat was Bailey.

* *

I am informed that the suggested dates for the final are August 22nd and 23rd, but it has not yet been decided whether the match shall be played on Parker's Piece or the University Ground.

CAMBRIDGE EXPRESS: SATURDAY 10 AUGUST 1895-2.

COUNTY v. BOROUGH POLICE.

The annual match between cricketing teams representing the Borough and County of Cambridge Police Forces took place on Wednesday, when the policemen journeyed to Royston and spent a pleasant time on the Heath. The County went first to the wicket, and made 50, to which p.-c. F. Brooks largely contributed. The Borough team scored 68. Then followed a piece of good cricket, in which the brothers p.c.'s F. and G. Brooks hit away in dashing style, and they were so well supported by Inspector Webb and p.-c. Carter that 09 runs for the loss of three wickets were registered quickly, after which stumps were drawn. The Borough thus obtained a victory on the first innings The bowling was chiefly notable for p.-c. Rackham's analysis of 6 for 15 runs for the Borough, and p.-c. F. Brooks' 6 for 25 for the County. At the close of play the party sat down to lunch at the White Horse. The following is the score:—

SPORT AND PLAY.

GOSSIP UPON CURRENT SPORTS AND PASTIMES.

A well-known writer on sport sends me the following particulars of K. S. Ranjitsinhji:—In a memorable cricket season the performances of K. S. Ranjitsinhji (a name famous Issac prefers to be known, instead of as Prince) have created almost as much attention as the doings of the great 'W. G.' "Ranji," as he is familiarly called in University cricket, came over from India some few seasons ago, and lived for a year or so with the Rev. Louis Brooks in preparation for University College. In October, 1889, he matriculated as a Non-Coll. in the University of Cambridge, but, though taking to cricket, few opportunities existed themselves for displaying his abilities. In due course he entered the foundation of Trinity College, but excepting an occasional match for the 2nd Eleven he was little known in the cricket world until he became associated with Cassandra and other Town Clubs, when he immediately came into prominence and a great future was predicted for him. In the year 1892, neither 'Varsity nor College could be induced to recognise his sterling qualities. However, during that year he had a magnificent average, and totalled over 2,000 runs, topping the century on 10 or 11 different occasions.

From the commencement of 1893 season his exceptional prowess demanded the attention of the 'Varsity authorities, and Mr. Jackson awarded him his blue, but although he averaged 22·79 in 'Varsity matches, in the Oxford and Cambridge match he failed, as so many other great cricketers have done. That year he was a regular member of the Trinity 1st Eleven, and came out with the fine figures of 71·8, eclipsing the batting achievements of Messrs. Jackson, W. G. Druce, Wells, &c. His chief scores this season were 119, 111, 101, and '02 not out. He also played for the County of Cambs. in the '93 and '94 seasons, but from want of an extensive programme and other causes did not shine very conspicuously. He played in several first-class matches last season and came out eighth, with an average of 32·57 for 10 innings, and altogether scored five centuries, viz., 129, 146 not out, 137 not out, 123 not out, 115 not out, and 103 not out. While known but little in first-class matches, his services during last winter were sought by several counties, but having a residential qualification for Sussex he declined to throw in his lot with that county. It will suffice to record the fact that this season, up to August 3rd, he had totalled in all matches 2,117 runs, with an average of over 70, while in first-class matches up to the date mentioned he averaged 69·23, with an aggregate of 1,497, and the following centuries figure in the large total:— 206 not out, 174 not out, 150, 137 not out, 110, and 100. The last four centuries are first-class Space does not permit me to mention other feats done with various other Clubs to which he belongs. On his day he is able to send down a good ball, and his name has been established through his brilliant fielding at short slip.

K. S. Ranjitsinhji, born in September, 1879, is the second son of the Jain of Nawanager, and is, therefore, in his 23rd year. His principal home in this country is Cambridge, where, during the winter months, he diligently participates in hunting and fishing. He is also a very good billiard player, and good judges have asserted that had he have taken more closely to tennis and athletics he would have represented his 'Varsity against Oxford in either pastime.

Ranjitsinhji, whose mode of living is an example which might well be followed by a number of cricketers, being a non-smoker and almost total abstainer, at the same time is one of the most jovial and unassuming fellows to meet; a keen supporter of all legitimate recreation, a severe critic on cricket, and very enthusiastic in all sport he indulges in.

Whether K. S. Ranjitsinhji returns to his native country at the end of this season remains to be seen. However, it is contended that he is too far infatuated and too great an exponent of cricket to be able to tear himself from the game in which he is so conspicuous at the present moment, and the wish of all sportsman is at least England will witness his prowess for many years to come.

The County of Cambs. Cricket Eleven cannot yet steer clear of rough luck, and had it been possible to complete the return match with Marylebone possibly they would not have experienced considerable trouble in saving the game. Had the Marylebone Club batted under the same conditions as Cambridge, they would not have made 231. The two Batters (who are likely to be most useful for the County), Barnes 119, and W. G. Grace, jun., who captured the M.C.C. eleven, bowled with success.

Shortly the final tie for the Cambs. Cricket Association Cup will be played, the contesting Clubs being the holders—the Old Higher Grade—and the Camden. Both Clubs command the respect of all votaries of the game, and the victory of either will be popular. The Camden, it is stated, have never given the Association any trouble, and have always been loyal to the Committee. They have frequently figured in the final stages, and are fully deserving of meeting with success. This equally applies to the "Old Boys," for whom P. Cornell has accomplished some truly wonderful feats with the ball of late, capturing six wickets for one run and seven for ten.

For them P. Luft is also a useful performer, and many think both he and Cornell should have received trials for the County. For the Camden, O'Connor is the main cause for hope. Against Hunts. the county "pro." actually captured 11 wickets for 7 runs. Were he to be able to repeat his best efforts the "Old Boys" would have anything but an easy task.

Several fairly good bats appear for each eleven, and everything points to an interesting game, and we hope the game will permit of the best side winning.

K. S. Ranjitsinhji informs me he will not be able to play local cricket till after the Sussex programme is completed, when he hopes to be able to take part in afternoon matches.

The changes from the previous week were that A. E. Stoddart and G. Brann qualified for the first dozen, H. W. Bainbridge and H. T. Hewett making way. The old Ex. man, however, is the third Cambridge man to score 1,000 runs in first-class matches. Such a feat may have previously been accomplished, but I never heard of it, and F. S. Jackson was the only Cambridge man to reach those figures last year with 1020 for 30 innings. W. N. F. Druce, Lord Hawke, Hayward, Carpenter, and F. S. Jackson have several more matches to play, it is quite possible they may join Messrs. Ranjitsinhji, Woods, and Bainbridge.

There is every prospect of Tom Hayward going out to South Africa with George Lohmann's team at the end of the season. I hear Tom is anxious to bring a team down to Cambridge at the end of the season. There was some talk of a match last April between K. S. Ranjitsinhji's XI. and Tom Hayward's. Such a fixture in September, with strong sides, should prove a great attraction.

STOCKPORT V. WERNETH.
Stockport met Werneth, on the ground of the latter, for the first time this season before a grand gate of over two thousand spectators, and a glorious morning weather. Both teams well represented. Werneth were minus the services of Wharmby, one of their pros, but had Brookes, of the Manchester staff and late of Stockport, assisting them, and who did very well too, taking five wickets for just over nine runs a piece. The visitors were also being fortunate in wining the toss sent in Redfern and Haggas to face the bowling of Brown and Fred Torkington. Redfern scored a boundary each off Torkington, but the score had only reached 22 when Haggas was grandly caught by Harry Royle at square leg. 22—1—10. Hall, a somewhat dashing pro, came next, but after scoring a single he was magnificently caught at slips by F. Tyler, 25—2—1. Heyworth followed in and made some good cricket for a ton. The fifth man, Bates, though Brown troubled him with his trickiness with the ball. At 46 a change of bowling was tried, F. Tyler going on for F. Torkington. The separation, however, came from the other end, Sam Brown bowling Heyworth with a grand ball, after that player had scored 14 in the stv. 53—5—14. Hearts shortly fell 5, Sam Brown being almost unplayable, the whole side being dismissed for the neat over amount of 95. This was due to the splendid bowling of Sam, whose analysis read—21.1 overs for 8 maidens 25 runs and 7 wickets. The fielding of the Stockport men was very, very good, all the catches that were took being very fine. Stockport opened their venture by sen ling in W. Hartley and J. Bates to the bowling of Hall and Brookes. The start was somewhat unfortune, losing Bates and Arthur Torkington with only 15 runs on the board. Brown and W. Hartley soon after the victims, and the good looking was going for 27 runs, but when Harold Hartley and Disney got not together a different complexion was put on the game, and they went at the bowling with the utmost confidence, especially the former, the 8th wicket falling at 85. Disney being stumped just as the next joined, Hartley, was playing a splendid game, and he also found a good partner in Long, who also played very well. Hartley, after playing a splendid game, was most unfortunate in playing on. After making 27 he was loudly cheered on returning, the sixth wicket thus falling at 83. Little needs for further comment except the batting of Fred Long and Frank Tyler, the former scoring 14 and the latter 12, not out; the innings closing for 110—Stockport winning by just a dozen. It was a grand match, Stockport fought out right to the nut very determinedly. Great praise is due to Harold Hartley, Fred Long, and Frank Tyler for the splendid way they played, all wickets up against the fine bowling of the tricky Hall and Brookes; and Stockport have every reason to be proud of that great victory, every man being a thorough trier. STOCKPORT V. WERNETH (2nd Eleven).—These two old rivals met for the first time this season at Heaton Lane, with glorious weather, and before a fair attendance. The visitors, winning the toss, decided to bat a little after three o'clock, in the bowling of Jones, who opened the innings coped led splendidly with a four, but on the whole runs came slowly, eight wickets being down for 53, when Marsland and Watson became partners and a splendid stand was made, the score being taken to 170 before either were separated, the score being still the same when the innings closed. After the usual interval, Stockport commenced to bat at a quarter past five, with a rather formidable task before them. May soon set to work admirably. The first wicket compiled 47, Brookes the number, May, the retiring batsman, accounting for 43 of these. F. Selby, the new man in, seemed rather uncomfortable at first, but began asserting good qualities when he got the service of Royle, the score reading 73—2—19. Marshall joined Selby with almost a hundred runs wanting, and the hand of the clock pointing to six. They had much to do, but the batsmen soon set to work, Marshall hitting four 4's in one over. Excitement ran high all except on. The spectators became quite glued to the game, especially the junior portion, who were enthusiastic with delight as each batsman sent the ball to the boundary. It was a treat to watch Marshall play each fresh bowlers. Nothing seemed to come amiss, but another wicket fell before the required runs came reached—Selby being bowled by the captain for a very useful 46. With ten minutes left to get the remaining nine runs, Jones joined Marshall, who had been going well, pulling out with a boundary. He was shortly afterwards bowled for a grand 57, comprising 13 fours and 4 twos. Time arrived—Marshall 8. off—Stockport were victorious by 11 runs and five wickets. Werneth was very strongly represented. This was their first defeat of the season. Next week Stockport are to come yet. Next week Stockport 2nd journey to Cheetham Hill, and it is to be hoped the match will be as successful as they were on Saturday. Score:—Werneth: H. Jackson b McLachlan 33, F. Dixon b Jones 4, J. GREAT MOOR V. BRAMALL.—Bramall Little End. In a most interesting game Great Moor won by 26 runs. The home team made a bad start, losing six wickets for 39 runs. A recovery was affected, mainly by Hopwood and Marriott, Ellis and Kemp who scored 39 not out by good and sound cricket. Hazel Grove also started badly, losing three wickets for six runs, but 50 runs were made for the fourth wicket; the eighth wicket fell for 59 runs, and the tenth for 79. STOCKPORT V. WERNETH.
Stockport met Werneth, on the ground of the latter club, the first time this season, before a grand gate of over two thousand spectators, and in charming weather. Both teams were well represented. Werneth were minus the services of Wharmby, one of their pros, but had Brookes, of the Manchester staff and late of Stockport, assisting them, and Brown was to the fore, too, taking five wickets for just over nine runs a piece. The Werneth captain being fortunate in winning the toss sent in Redfern and Haggas to face the bowling of Brown and Fred Torkington. Both men scored a boundary each off Torkington, but the score had only reached 22 when Haggas was grandly caught by Harry Royle at square leg, 22—1—40. Hall, the popular Werneth pro, came next, but, after scoring a couple he was marvellously caught at slips by F. Tyler, 26—2—1. Heyworth followed in and here some good cricket was seen. Redfern played well against Brown, but Hall was with his trickiness with the ball. At 46 a change of bowling was tried, F. Tyler going on for F. Torkington. The same result nearly came from the other end, Sam Brown bowling Heyworth with a grand ball, after that player had scored 14 in nice style, 53—3—4. After this wickets fell fast, Sam Brown being almost unplayable, the whole side being dismissed for the not very grand score of 98. This was due to the splendid bowling of Sam, whose analysis read—21 1 overs for 8 maidens 25 runs and 7 wickets. The fielding of the Stockport men was also very good, all the catches that were took being very fine. Stockport opened their venture by sending in W. Hartley and J. Bates to the bowling of Hall and Brookes. The start was at some direction losing Bates and Arthur Torkington with only 15 runs on the board. Brown and W. Hartley soon fallen victims, and fore good wickets were down for 27 runs, but when Harold Hartley and Disney got together a different complexion was put on the game, and they treated the bowling of with almost confidence, especially the former, the fifth wicket falling at 53, Disney being bowled. Fred Long next joined Hartley, who was playing a splendid game, and he also found a good partner in Long, who also played very well. Hartley, after playing a splendid game, was most unfortunate in playing on. After making 27 he was boldly accused on returning, the sixth wicket thus falling at 89. Little praise for further occurred, except the batting of Fred Long and Frank Tyler, the former scoring 14 and the latter 12, not out ; the innings closing for 110—Stockport winning by just a dozen. It was a grand match, and was fought out right up to the end intermittently. Great praise is due to Harold Hartley, Fred Long, and Frank Tyler for the splendid way they kept their wickets up against the fine bowling of the tricky Hall and Brookes ; and Stockport have every reason to be proud of their handsome victory, every man being a thorough trier. Below is appended full score and analysis.

STOCKPORT v. WERNETH (2nd Eleven)—These two old rivals met for the first time this season at Cale Green Park, in glorious weather, and before a large attendance of spectators, who, unluckily, decided to bat a little after three o'clock, to the bowling of Jones and McLachlan. Jackson opened splendidly with a four, but on the whole runs came slowly, eight wickets being down for 93, when Marsland and Watson became partners and a splendid stand was made, the score being taken to 170 before either were separated, the score being still the same when the innings closed. After the interval Stockport commenced to bat at about a quarter past five, with a rather formidable task before them. May soon set to work in earnest. The first wicket compiled 57 against the bowling of Jones the retiring batsman, accounting for 43 of these. F. Selby, the new man in, seemed rather uncomfortable as yet he was getting more at home when he lost the service of Royle, the score reading 73—2—19. Marshall joined Selby with almost a hundred runs in sight, and the figures on the clock pointing to six. They had much to do, but the batsmen soon set to work, Marshall hitting four 4's in one over. Excitement ran high in both camps but The Werneth became quite devoted to the game, especially the junior portion, who were enthusiastic with delight as each batsman saw the leather to the boundary. It was grand to watch Marshall play each from bowler. Nothing seemed to come amiss, but an ther wicket fall before the required runs were reached, thus Selby being bowled, leaving the captain for a very useful 46. With ten minutes left to get the remaining nine runs, Jones joined Marshall, the latter soon settling the winning hit with a boundary. He was shortly afterwards bowled for a grand 57, comprising 13 fours and 4 twos. This arrived when the score 170 not out—Stockport being victorious by 11 runs and five wickets. Werneth was very strongly represented. This was their first defeat of the season ; and though they will have to come yet. Next week Stockport 2nd Journey to Cheetham Hill, and it is to be hoped that team will be as successful as they were on Saturday. Score:—Werneth: H. Jackson b McLachlan 35, F. Dixon b Jones 4, J. CHESHIRE COUNTY NEWS: FRIDAY 2 AUGUST 1895-2

SATURDAY'S MATCHES.
Castleton v. Stockport.

This match should have been played at Castleton, but owing to the ground being unfit caused through the heavy rains it was cancelled. Next week the Stockport team journey to Bolton, to try conclusions with the town club.

ST. THOMAS' ATHLETIC v. NORBURY 2ND.—At Norbury. The home team batted first, scoring 35 all out. J. Bowden took seven wickets for five runs and W. Burt three for eight. J. Garner and J. Bowden went in to bat for St. Thomas'. They had scored 26 when rain stopped the match, leaving St. Thomas' winners by 10 wickets and 14 runs. FINAL TIE.
STOCKPORT CONGREGATIONAL v. REDDISH ST. ELIZABETH'S.—This interesting event, for a beautiful shield presented by Mr. Sydney Hollins, was commenced on the football ground adjoining the Stockport Cricket Ground on Saturday afternoon. The pitch, which had been prepared for the occasion, was saturated by the rain, and all in favour of the bowlers. The Congregational have vanquished the Lads' Club and St. Peter's, whilst for St. Elizabeth's position they have had to meet and defeat the Lads' Club 2nd and last year's winners, Hanover. The Congregational batted first, sending in Dowbiggin and H. Jebson, they having to face the bowling of Hardman and Hunt. Both batsmen hit well. The innings only realised 49.
